рд╕рд╛рдпрдг-рднрд╛рд╖реНрдпрдореН

тАШ рдпреЛрдирд┐рдГ’ рдЗрддрд┐ рдирд╡рд░реНрдЪрдореН рдПрдХрд╛рджрд╢рдВ рд╕реВрдХреНрддрдВ рдХреБрд╕реНрд╕рд╕реНрдпрд╛рд░реНрд╖рдВ рддреНрд░реИрд╖реНрдЯреБрднрдореН рдРрдиреНрджреНрд░рдореН ред ‘рдпреЛрдирд┐рд░реНрдирд╡’ рдЗрддреНрдпрдиреБрдХреНрд░рд╛рдиреНрддрдореН ред рд╕реВрдХреНрддрд╡рд┐рдирд┐рдпреЛрдЧреЛ рд▓реИрдЩреНрдЧрд┐рдХрдГ рее

Jamison Brereton

104
Indra
Kutsa ─Ас╣Еgirasa
9 verses: triс╣гс╣нubh
At nine verses, this final hymn of the Kutsa Indra cycle is longer than its immediate predecessor by one verse. On this basis Oldenberg identified it as an Anhangslied (a supplementary hymn appended to the end of a divinity cycle), an opinion sup ported by the fact that, alone of the Kutsa hymns (I.94тАУ98, 100тАУ115), it lacks the Kutsa refrain always found in the last hemistich of the last verse of each hymn. (This applies only to trimeter hymns; the g─Бyatr─л hymn I.97, not surprisingly, also lacks it.) But if тАЬsupplementaryтАЭ suggests something attenuated or in decline, that suggestion is emphatically false. This hymn is extraordinary complex and challeng ing, especially in its mythological portion (vss. 3тАУ5), and we are far from solving the many puzzles it presents. It may have been inserted just here in the Saс╣Гhit─Б because the mythological portion in part concerns a foe named Kuyava, or the demon тАЬof bad grain,тАЭ that is, bad harvest, mentioned as having been slain by Indra in the last verse of the preceding hymn (I.103.8ab). There are also several other lexical connec tions between I.103 and this hymn, particularly the two forms of ┼Ыr├бd-dh─Б тАЬtrustтАЭ in each hymn, which may have encouraged the insertion, or even the composition, of this supplementary poem.
As in many hymns, an invitation to Indra to come to the sacrifice is found in the first verse. But some of the features of this verse give us a taste of what is to

come. For example, the apparently straightforward simile in p─Бda b, rendered here as тАЬlike a steed sounding off,тАЭ is actually a triple pun (impossible to convey in translation): the participle suv─Бna╠Бс╕е can belong to three different verbs: тАЬsound,тАЭ тАЬimpel,тАЭ and тАЬpress (of soma).тАЭ When Indra takes his seat on the ritual ground, he is compared to a horse neighing, to a horse that has been driven to the place where it takes its rest, and finally to soma (which itself is often compared to a horse) being pressed in its womb. In fact the same phraseology is applied to soma elsewhere (cf. IX.70.7).
The second verse introduces the theme that will dominate the mythological por tion, the importance of having Indra and the other gods on our side in the battle against the traditional, perhaps indigenous, enemies of the ─Аrya, identified as D─Бsa (vs. 2) and Dasyu (vs. 5). The word тАЬcolorтАЭ in the final p─Бda of the verse is a fre quent way of referring to a cohort, a unified group of people. Our urgent need for Indra and his aid returns after the mythological portion, in fact starting with the second half of verse 5. The poet twice asserts his trust in IndraтАЩs power (6d, 7a); this may not be a mere repetition but refer rather to the two aspects of IndraтАЩs power, as described in the first verse of the preceding hymn (I.103.1), partly in heaven and partly on earth. And he also issues a series of peremptory commands, especially negative commands, to Indra from verse 5ab through verse 8, which ends with a plea not to split our тАЬeggsтАЭ or our тАЬcups,тАЭ in our opinion male and female genitalia respectively. The final verse (9) is again a simple invitation to soma.
The problematic verses 3тАУ5ab need to be interpreted within this outer frame work, the context of IndraтАЩs martial aid to the embattled ─Аrya. Our interpretation of these verses is quite speculative and differs from those of others, but we have attempted to construct a coherent account, while supplying as little extraneous material as possible. What is immediately striking about the first of these verses (3) is the presence of two female figures, the тАЬtwo maidens of Kuyava,тАЭ who are obviously rivers. More rivers appear in the second half of the next verse (4cd). Now the advance of the ─Аrya is often obstructed by rivers, which must be forded (see esp. III.33), and important battles are fought beside these barrier rivers (see esp. VII.18). The two maidens of Kuyava thus could be rivers initially controlled by forces impeding the progress of the ─Аrya. The charming image of the foamy riv
ers тАЬbathing in milkтАЭ turns swiftly cruel: тАЬmay they both be smashed,тАЭ perhaps a reference to their violent confluence with a mightier river still, the ┼Ъiph─Б (otherwise unknown). This verse reminds us of V.30.9, in another Indra hymn, in which Indra confronts an enemy who тАЬmade women his weaponsтАЭ and who has two streams, perhaps the same rivers as here. Once Indra recognizes the situation, he initiates battle.

30.9 Because the D─Бsa made women his weapons, what can they do to me? His armies lack strength.

Since he [=Indra] distinguished both his [=D─БsaтАЩs?] streams, therefore Indra advanced on the Dasyu to fight. The point may be that though rivers are formidable natural obstructions, they are also, by grammatical gender, feminine, and females are, by nature, weak. Conceiving of rivers as women robs them of their power and allows them to be overcome. The analogue of IndraтАЩs distinguishing the streams in V.30.9 may be found in our verse 5ab, where the тАЬguideтАЭ of the Dasyu, which we interpret both physically as the streambed and metaphorically as his strategy, is revealed. It is another female who reveals and recognizes it, and, on the basis of shared lexicon, we think that Dawn is the figure lurking behind this unidentified feminine. Dawn regularly makes things visible and knows the way forward, and here she leads the Aryan forces to the very seat of the enemy.
These ─Аrya forces are personified in ─Аyu in verse 4ab. His тАЬnavelтАЭ has been effaced for two reasons. On the one hand, the mythic hero ─Аyu is the son of the Apsaras Urva┼Ы─л and the mortal king Pur┼лravas, but his lot was cast with mortals when his mother abandoned him (see X.95), and in this sense his true origins have been, at the least, muddied. But there is a more positive aspect to this phrase; since he and the other ─Аrya continue to move forward into ever new territory, where they came from recedes into the background: their future is forward.
What remains to be explained is the emphasis on the riversтАЩ тАЬcarrying awayтАЭ (├бva-тИЪbhR╠е3a, 3b; тИЪbhR╠е4b). Here we feel there may be a latent ritual reference. The тАЬfinal bathтАЭ (avabhr╠еth├б, a derivative of ├бva-тИЪbhr╠е) of the sacrificer and his wife at the end of the classical ┼Ыrauta ritual is purificatory, removing all ritual pollution. And it is a commonplace in Vedic that rivers and moving waters in general carry away stain. The mention of bathing in 3c, combined with the emphasis on тАЬcarry
ing away,тАЭ suggests that a notion of expiation and ritual cleansing has been super imposed on this battle scene. What is not clear is what is being carried away. Is it pollution produced by some offense of the enemy? An even more speculative idea is inspired by the mention of тАЬfoamтАЭ (ph├йnam 3b): in a well-known myth Indra cuts off the head of the demon Namuci with the foam of waters (see VIII.14.13), having tricked him by forswearing all conventional weapons. It is possible that the rivers here are carrying away the defilement that Indra acquired from this underhanded killing. It is worthy of note that the Namuci myth is treated in V.30.7тАУ8, right before the verse cited above about enemy streams.
The last word has not been said about this dense and fascinating hymn, but we hope to have advanced the discussion.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Wilson
English translation:

тАЬThe altar has been raised, Indra, for your seat; hasten to sit upon it as a neighing horse (hastens to his stable); slackening the reins, and letting your coursers free, who, at the season of sacrifice, bear you night and day.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Added: y─Бgak─Бle pr─Бpte = the time of sacrifice having arrived; the text has only, ‘for the season of sacrifice’;

Prapitve = pr─Бpte = lit. arrived, or adversb signifying proximity: near, nigh, at hand (Nirukta 3.20)

Jamison Brereton

A womb has been prepared for you to sit down in, Indra. Sit down in it here, like a steed sounding off,
once you have released your vitality [/the birds] and unhitched the horses, who convey you so well to the mealtime, evening and morning.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction, s u v─Бn├│ n─Б╠Бrv─Б in b contains a triple pun, since the participle s u v─Бn├бс╕е can be read in three different ways: 1) as nom. sg. to the adj.

sv─Бn├б- тАШsoundingтАЩ (тИЪsvan тАШsoundтАЩ)(so Oldenberg, Renou, Witzel Got┼Н); 2) as athem. med. part. to тИЪs┼л тАШimpelтАЩ, used passively (so Geldner); 3) as athem. med. part. to тИЪsu тАШpressтАЩ, also used passively (mentioned by Geldner in n. 1b). In this last case the simile would compare Indra seating himself on the ritual ground to soma placed at the soma press. The diction in the rest of this hemistich is quite similar to that found in soma hymns. Cf., e.g., IX.70.7 ─Б╠Бy├│niс╣Г s├│maс╕е s├║krta╠е с╣Г n├н с╣г─лdati, with y├│ni-, a form of тИЪkс╣Ы characterizing it, and the verb n├н тИЪsad. In the published translation only the first of these is given, because including a set of bracketed alternatives – “[/ like a steed having been impelled [/ like (soma) the steed being pressed]” – seemed disruptive and distracting.

P─Бda b contains the word v├бyaс╕е, which raises the same question as in 103.7: is this the plural to v├н- тАШbirdтАЩ or the neuter sg. s-stem v├бyas- тАШvitality, vigorтАЩ? Neither of them is an entirely natural object to vim├║cy─Б тАШhaving releasedтАЩ in this context. Geldner (/Witzel Got┼Н) opt for the latter, Oldenberg and Renou for the birds. In the published translation I treat it as a pun, but I am not certain what either phrase would represent.

In d v├бh─лyasaс╕е is of course a comparative, but, again, so rendering it would be awkward.

Griffith

THE altar hath been made for thee to rest on: come like a panting courser and be seated.
Loosen thy flying Steeds, set free thy Horses who bear thee swiftly nigh at eve and morning.

Geldner

Ein Scho├Я ist f├╝r dich, Indra, zum Niedersitzen bereitet. Auf ihn setz dich nieder, zum Laufe getrieben wie ein Rennpferd, die Kraft ausspannend, die Rosse anhaltend, die vor Essenszeit bei Dunkelwerden und morgens am besten ziehen!

Grassmann

Hier ist der Schooss zum Sitzen dir bereitet, zur Einkehr l├╢se deine Fl├╝gelrosse, Die trefflich fahren fr├╝h, am Tag, am Abend, und setz dich, Indra, wie ein Renner schnaufend.

Elizarenkova

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рдЕрдиреНрд╡рдпрдГ

English translation:

тАЬThese person ns have come to Indra (to solicit) his protection; may he quickly direct them on the way; may the gods repress the wrathof the destroyer, and bring to our solemnity the obviator of evil.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

These men here have come to Indra for help. Even now, immediately, he should go to them along the roads.
The gods will extinguish the battle-fury of the D─Бsa. They will convey our тАЬcolorтАЭ to welfare.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Unlike the standard tr. I take the two acc. plurals in b, t─Б╠Бn тАж ├бdhvanaс╕е, separately, with the first referring to the тАШmenтАЩ (n├бraс╕е) of p─Бda a. One might object that since they тАЬhave comeтАЭ (guс╕е) to him in that p─Бda, he has no need to go to them in the next, but, at least in English, тАЬgo to for helpтАЭ is tantamount to тАЬask for helpтАЭ and need not involve any actual travel on the part of the men. And in any case he would need to join them at the place of battle. I.71.9, which contains the phrase ├бdhvanaс╕е sadh├б ├йti without t─Б╠Бn, may support me.

For the pf. opt. jagamy─Бt, see Jamison 2009 (East and West); as exhaustively demonstrated there, the pf. opt. has no special тАЬperfectтАЭ value and here means simply тАЬshould go.тАЭ The published translation reflects the emendation of ┼Ыcamnan to *┼Ыamnan, suggested by Grassmann and argued for in Jamison 1983: 103 n. 62. I am now less certain about this emendation than I was then, being more sympathetic to OldenbergтАЩs questioning how this corruption could have arisen. On the other hand, the Aves. gerundive sc─Е╧С╬▓a- (V.13.40), adduced by Geldner as a cognate to a supposed root тИЪ┼Ыcam, provides no support for a separate etymon of this shape, since it is, with Insler, better attributed to the root *skand тАШbreakтАЩ, well attested in Avestan and elsewhere in Iranian. For details see Jamison 1983 loc. cit.

Griffith

These men have come to Indra for assistance: shall he not quickly come upon these pathways?
May the Gods quell the fury of the Dasa, and may they lead our folk to happy fortune.

Zu Indra eilten labend diese M├дnner, er komme nun sogleich auf diesen Wegen; Des D├дmons Wuth beschwichtigen die G├╢tter, zum Gl├╝cke m├╢gen unsern Stamm sie f├╝hren.

Wilson
English translation:

тАЬ(The Asura), knowing the wealth of others, carries it off of himself; present in the water, he carries off, of himself, the foam; the two wives of Kuyava bathe with the water; may they be drowned in the depths of the ┼Ъiph─Б river.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

(The Asura): named Kuyava

Jamison Brereton

She who knows his [=KuyavaтАЩs?] will carries away by herself; (the other) by herself carries away the foam in her water.
The two maidens of Kuyava bathe in milk: may they both be smashed in the torrent of the ┼Ъiph─Б (River).

Jamison Brereton Notes

For my general interpr. of this verse see published introduction. I will not discuss the various other interpretations in detail here or repeat what I said in the introduction. I will point out that most tr. take the subject of both a and b to be the ┼Ъiph─Б river in 4, but this assumes that a and b are essentially repetitions of each other, which would constitute an unartful duplication in a very artful hymn. Since p─Бdas cd contain dual feminines, it makes more sense to take ab as an implicit тАЬthe one тАж the otherтАЭ construction, with the two subjects the same as the duals in cd. One problem with this interpr. is that the verb ├бva тАж bharate in p─Бda a lacks an overt object; I suggest in the introduction. that it might be defilement or pollution that is also removed ritually by the avabhс╣Ыth├б bath at the end of the classic Vedic sacrifice.

In d we find a periphrastic passive construction: hat├й тАж sy─Бt─Бm тАЬmay the two be smashed.тАЭ Though this periphrasis is somewhat unusual, even a stray thought of what the 3rd du. middle opt. of the passive of тИЪhan would be (*hanyey─Бt─Бm) may explain the substitution. According to Macdonell (VG), such forms are not attested in Vedic.

Griffith

He who hath only wish as his possession casts on himself, casts foam amid the waters.
Both wives of Kuyava in milk have bathed them: may they be drowned within the depth of Sipha.

Geldner

Sie f├╝hrt selbst den Schaum mit sich, die Absicht erratend, sie f├╝hrt selbst den Schaum auf ihrem Wasser mit sich: In Milch baden des Kuyava beide Weiber; die sollen in der Str├╢mung der Sipha get├╢tet werden.

Grassmann

Von selbst vergeht, wer l├╝stern ist nach G├╝tern, von selbst vergeht der Schaum auf Wasserwogen, Kujava’s Weiber baden sich im Milchtrank, zerschmettert sein sie in der ├Зipha Stromfall.

English translation:

тАЬThe abiding-plural ce of the vagrant (Kuyava) was concealed (in the midst) of the water; the hero increases with the waters formerly (carried off), and is renowned (throughout the world); the A├▒jas─л, kuli┼Ы─л and V─лrapatni rivers, plural asing him with their substance, sustain him with their waters.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Vagrant: Aya is an appellative of Kuyava; from ay, to go–one going about to do mischief to others

Jamison Brereton

The navel [=lineage, origin] of the ─Аyu to come has been effaced. He lengthens his life through the (dawns) in front. The champion reigns. The A├▒jas─л, the Kuli┼Ы─л, (the Sarasvat─л) whose husband is the virile oneтАФ they, impelling the milk, carry it with their waters.

Likewise consult the published introduction. for my interpr. of this verse.

English translation:

тАЬSince the track that leads to the dwelling of the Dasyu has been seen by us, as a cow knows the way to her stall, therefore do you, Maghavan, (defend us) from his repeated violence; do not you cast away as a libertine throws away wealth.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Of the Dasyu: Of Kuyava

Jamison Brereton

When that guide [=both riverbed and strategy] of the Dasyu was revealed, she who recognized it [=Dawn] went to his seat as if
straight home.
Now then, bounteous one, just grant us acclaim. DonтАЩt hand us over, like a careless man his bounties.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Again, consult the published introduction. As noted there, I think the unidentified female in b is Dawn, who leads the ─Аrya forces across the river(s) and against the Dasyu.

The other standard tr. take her rather as Saram─Б, IndraтАЩs canine sidekick, on the fragile basis of III.31.6: the only point of contact between the two passages being the rather generic j─Бnat─л╠Бg─Бt тАЬrecognizing, she went.тАЭ I see no reason to introduce Saram─Б here; Dawn has been anticipated by the apparent reference to the dawns in 4b (p┼л╠Бrv─Бbhiс╕е), and the revealing (├бdar┼Ыi – a standard item of Uс╣гas vocabulary) of the DasyuтАЩs streambed/strategy could easily happen at dawn.

In d niс╣гс╣гap├нn- is a hapax, but тАШwithout care, carelessтАЩ (тИЪsap тАШserve, care forтАЩ) makes sense.

Wilson
English translation:

тАЬExcite in us, Indra, veneration for the sun, for the waters, and for those who are worthy of the praise of living beings, as exempt from sin; injure not our offspring while yet in the womb, for our trust is in yourmighty power.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

You, IndraтАФgive us a share in the sun, in the waters, in blamelessness, in the praise of the living.
Do no harm to our inner delight. Trust has been placed in your great Indrian power.

Griffith

Indra, as such, give us a share of sunlight, of waters, sinlessness, and reputation.
Do thou no harm to our yet unborn offspring: our trust is in thy mighty Indra-power.

Geldner

La├Я du, Indra, uns am Sonnenlicht, am Wasser, an der Schuldlosigkeit, an der Rede der Lebenden teilhaben. Sch├дdige nicht unsere Herzensfreude! Man hat deinem gro├Яen indrischen Namen vertraut.

Grassmann

O Indra, schenk uns Sonnenlicht und Wasser, Schuldlosigkeit verleih und Lob der Menschen, Verdirb uns nicht die Freude, die uns nah ist, wir trauen deiner grossen Indrast├дrke.

Elizarenkova

Wilson
English translation:

тАЬHence, Indra, I meditate on you; on his (your power) has our trust been plural ced; showerer (of benefits), direct us to great wealth; consign us not, you who are invoked by many, to a destitute dwelling; give, Indra, food and drink to the hungry.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

And, I think, trust has been placed in this (power) of yours right here. As bull, bestir yourself toward great stakes.
DonтАЩt (put) us in an unprepared womb, o much invoked one. Indra, grant vitality and the pressing (of soma) to those who hunger
for them.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The ┼Ыr├бd тИЪdh─Б lexeme is repeated here from the immediately preceding p─Бda (6d; cf. also I.103.3, 5). Curiously the announced тАЬtrustтАЭ seems to be undercut by manye тАЬI think.тАЭ The phrase te asmai expressing the recipient of the trust requires some comment. Geldner seems to take te as a dative, parallel to mahat├б indriy─Б╠Бya in 6d, and asmai adverbially (тАЬdaf├╝rтАЭ). But 6d also contains a te, which must be the genitive limiting the dat. phrase, and such an interpretation fits better here, with te dependent on asmai. What is the referent of asmai, whose referent should already be present in the discourse because of the lack of accent on asmai? The easiest interpretation is that it simply picks up indriy─Б╠Бya of 6d (so Renou), but again, as in 3ab, this would seem an unartful duplication. Although my interpr. requires taking into account a longer stretch of discourse, it avoids the repetitive scenario. In I.103.1 Indra is credited with indriy├б- in two locations, heaven and earth, and I think the dual nature of this indriy├б- is what is at issue here: the heavenly one in 6d, the one right here (hence the near-deictic asmai) in 7a. So although asmai picks up the indriy─Б╠Бya of 6d, it also refers to a different aspect of this referent.

Hoffmann (1967: 53, followed by Witzel Got┼Н) tentatively suggests taking cd as a single clause. This avoids the need to supply a verb in a, but the тАЬunprepared wombтАЭ (HoffmannтАЩs тАЬan unbereiteter St├дtteтАЭ) of c does not seem to have much to do semantically with d.

The object phrase v├бya ─Бsut├нm in d, in conjunction with the dat. part.

kс╣г├║dhyadbhyaс╕е тАШhungeringтАЩ, invites an interpretation of the pair as тАЬfood and drinkтАЭ (Geldner тАЬst├дrkende Speise und TrankтАЭ; Hoffmann just тАЬSpeise und TrankтАЭ; Witzel Got┼Н тАЬNahrung und TrankтАЭ). But the abstract nature of v├бyas- should be respected, and I also doubt that ─Бsut├н- is just any drink, rather than referring to the soma pressing. As for what it all means – I would suggest that cd be interpreted in the context of the rest of the hymn, particularly the outer framework depicting IndraтАЩs aid to the ─Аrya in conflict. They are here asking him not to drop them into battle without proper means (тАЬinto an unprepared wombтАЭ); the d p─Бda then expresses what they need: vital energy, that is, the physical and mental vigor required for combat, and the soma pressing, that is, the ritual means to attract Indra and secure his aid. Vs. 9 then issues the invitation to the soma drinking.

Griffith

Now we, I think, in thee as such have trusted: lead us on, Mighty One, to ample riches.
In no unready house give us, O Indra invoked of many, food and drink when hungry.

Geldner

Darum meine ich: Man hat dir daf├╝r Vertrauen geschenkt. Als Bulle mache dich zu gro├Яem Kampfgewinn scharf! Setz uns nicht in ein unfertiges Nest, du Vielgerufener! Indra, gib den Hungernden st├дrkende Speise und Trank!

Grassmann

So, mein’ ich, haben wir auf dich getrauet; f├╝hr als ein Held uns hin zu grossem Reichthum; Setz, vielgerufner, nicht in ├╢den Sitz uns, den Hungernden gib, Indra, Trank und Speise.

Elizarenkova

Wilson
English translation:

тАЬHarm us not, Indra, abandon us not; deprive us not of the enjoyments that aare dear to us; injure not, affluent ┼Ъakra, our unborn offspring; harm not those who are capable (only of crawling) on their knees.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

DonтАЩt smite us, Indra; donтАЩt hand us over. DonтАЩt steal our dear delights. DonтАЩt split apart our тАЬeggs,тАЭ o bounteous and powerful one; donтАЩt split our тАЬcupsтАЭ along with their contents.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This verse details various possible bad outcomes if they do not manage to secure his aid. As indicated in the published introduction, I take the тАЬeggsтАЭ (─Бс╣Зс╕Н─Б╠Б) and тАЬcupsтАЭ (p─Б╠Бtr─Б) as slangy references to male and female genitalia, or in the latter case perhaps better тАШwombsтАЩ. I tr. -j─Бnuс╣г─Бс╣Зi loosely тАШcontentsтАЩ, but if the тАЬcupsтАЭ are wombs, then тАШprogeny, offspring, broodтАЩ would work.

Griffith

Slay us not, Indra; do not thou forsake us: steal not away the joys which we delight in.
Rend not our unborn brood, strong Lord of Bounty! our vessels with the life that is within them.

Geldner

T├╢te uns nicht, Indra, gib uns nicht preis, raub uns nicht unsere lieben Gen├╝sse! Zerbrich nicht, du Gabenreicher, M├дchtiger, unsere Eier, zerbrich nicht die Schalen samt der Brut!

Grassmann

Erschlag’ uns nicht, o Indra, noch verwirf uns, und raube nicht uns unsre liebe Nahrung; Zerst├╢r uns, Indra, nicht das ungeborne, noch auch die Leiber, die die Frucht umschliessen.

Elizarenkova

Wilson
English translation:

тАЬCome into our presence; they have called you, fond of the Soma; it is prepared; drink of it for your exhilaration; vast of limb, distend your stomach, and when invoked hear us, as a father (listens to the words of his sons).тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

Come here nearby. They say you have lust for soma. Here it is,
pressed: drink it, for exhilaration.
Being of broad expanse, rain it into your belly. Like a father, hear us when you are being called on.

Griffith

Come to us; they have called thee Soma-lover: here is the pressed juice. Drink thereof for rapture.
Widely-capacious, pour it down within thee, and, invocated, hear us like a Father.

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдерднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - рдкреНрд░рдЬрд╛рдЬрдиреЛрдВ рдХреЛ рдЪрд╛рд╣рд┐рдпреЗ рдХрд┐ рд╕рднрд╛рдкрддрд┐ рдЖрджрд┐ рд░рд╛рдЬрдкреБрд░реБрд╖реЛрдВ рдХреЛ рдЦрд╛рди, рдкрд╛рди, рд╡рд╕реНрддреНрд░, рдзрди, рдпрд╛рди рдФрд░ рдореАрдареА-рдореАрдареА рдмрд╛рддреЛрдВ рд╕реЗ рд╕рджрд╛ рдЖрдирдиреНрджрд┐рдд рдмрдирд╛рдпреЗ рд░рд╣реЗрдВ рдФрд░ рд░рд╛рдЬрдкреБрд░реБрд╖реЛрдВ рдХреЛ рднреА рдЪрд╛рд╣рд┐рдпреЗ рдХрд┐ рдкреНрд░рдЬрд╛рдЬрдиреЛрдВ рдХреЛ рдкреБрддреНрд░ рдХреЗ рд╕рдорд╛рди рдирд┐рд░рдиреНрддрд░ рдкрд╛рд▓реЗрдВ рее реп реердЗрд╕ рд╕реВрдХреНрдд рдореЗрдВ рд╕рднрд╛рдкрддрд┐ рд░рд╛рдЬрд╛ рдФрд░ рдкреНрд░рдЬрд╛ рдХреЗ рдХрд░рдиреЗ рдпреЛрдЧреНрдп рд╡реНрдпрд╡рд╣рд╛рд░ рдХреЗ рд╡рд░реНрдгрди рд╕реЗ рдЗрд╕ рд╕реВрдХреНрдд рдХреЗ рдЕрд░реНрде рдХреА рдкреВрд░реНрд╡ рд╕реВрдХреНрдд рдХреЗ рдЕрд░реНрде рдХреЗ рд╕рд╛рде рд╕рдЩреНрдЧрддрд┐ рдЬрд╛рдирдиреА рдЪрд╛рд╣рд┐рдпреЗ реердпрд╣ резрежрек рдПрдХрд╕реМ рдЪрд╛рд░рд╡рд╛рдБ рд╕реВрдХреНрдд рдФрд░ резреп рдЙрдиреНрдиреАрд╕рд╡рд╛рдБ рд╡рд░реНрдЧ рдкреВрд░рд╛ рд╣реБрдЖ рее

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рдЕрдиреНрд╡рдпрдГ

рдЕрдиреНрд╡рдп: рд╣реЗ рд╕рднрд╛рдзреНрдпрдХреНрд╖ рдпрддрд╕реНрддреНрд╡рд╛ рддреНрд╡рд╛рдВ рд╕реЛрдордХрд╛рдордорд╛рд╣реБрд░рддрд╕реНрддреНрд╡рдорд░реНрд╡рд╛рдЩреЗрд╣рд┐ред рдЕрдпрдВ рд╕реБрддрд╕реНрддрд╕реНрдп рдорджрд╛рдп рдкрд┐рдмред рдЙрд░реБрд╡реНрдпрдЪрд╛рд╕реНрддреНрд╡рдВ рдЬрдард░реЗ рдЖрд╡реГрд╖рд╕реНрд╡ред рдЕрд╕реНрдорд╛рднрд┐рд░реНрд╣реВрдпрдорд╛рдирд╕реНрддреНрд╡рдВ рдкрд┐рддреЗрд╡ рдирдГ рд╢реГрдгреБрд╣рд┐ рее реп рее

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рд╡рд┐рд╖рдпрдГ

рдкреБрдирдГ рдкреНрд░рдЬрдпрд╛ рддреЗрди рд╕рд╣ рдХрд┐рдВ рдкреНрд░рддрд┐рдЬреНрдЮрд╛рддрд╡реНрдпрдорд┐рддреНрдпреБрдкрджрд┐рд╢реНрдпрддреЗ ред

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдерд╛рдиреНрд╡рдпрднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - (рдЕрд░реНрд╡рд╛рдЩреН) рдЕрд░реНрд╡рд╛рдЪреАрдиреЗ рд╡реНрдпрд╡рд╣рд╛рд░реЗ (рдЖ, рдЗрд╣рд┐) рдЖрдЧрдЪреНрдЫ (рд╕реЛрдордХрд╛рдордореН) рдЕрднрд┐рд╖реБрддрд╛рдирд╛рдВ рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдерд╛рдирд╛рдВ рд░рд╕рдВ рдХрд╛рдордпрддреЗ рдпрд╕реНрддрдореН (рддреНрд╡рд╛) рддреНрд╡рд╛рдореН (рдЖрд╣реБрдГ) рдХрдердпрдиреНрддрд┐ (рдЕрдпрдореН) рдкреНрд░рд╕рд┐рджреНрдзрдГ (рд╕реБрддрдГ) рдирд┐рд╖реНрдкрд╛рджрд┐рддрдГ (рддрд╕реНрдп) рддрдореНред рдЕрддреНрд░ рд╢реЗрд╖рддреНрд╡рд╡рд┐рд╡рдХреНрд╖рд╛рдпрд╛рдВ рдХрд░реНрдордгрд┐ рд╖рд╖реНрдареАред (рдкрд┐рдм) рдЕрддреНрд░ рджреНрд╡реНрдпрдЪреЛрд╜рддрд╕реНрддрд┐рдЩ рдЗрддрд┐ рджреАрд░реНрдШрдГред (рдорджрд╛рдп) рд╣рд░реНрд╖рд╛рдп (рдЙрд░реБрд╡реНрдпрдЪрд╛рдГ) рдЙрд░реБ рдмрд╣реБрд╡рд┐рдзрдВ рд╡реНрдпрдЪреЛ рд╡рд┐рдЬреНрдЮрд╛рдирдВ рдкреВрдЬрдирдВ рд╕рддреНрдХрд░рдгрдВ рд╡рд╛ рдпрд╕реНрдп рд╕рдГ (рдЬрдард░реЗ) рдЬрд╛рдпрдиреНрддреЗ рдпрд╕реНрдорд╛рджреБрджрд░рд╛рджреНрд╡рд╛ рддрд╕реНрдорд┐рдиреНред рдЬрдиреЗрд░рд░рд╖реНрда рдЪред рдЙреж релред рейреоред рдЕрддреНрд░ рдЬрди рдзрд╛рддреЛрд╜рд░рдГ рдкреНрд░рддреНрдпрдпреЛ рдирдХрд╛рд░рд╕реНрдп рдардХрд╛рд░рд╢реНрдЪред (рдЖ) (рд╡реГрд╖рд╕реНрд╡) рд╕рд┐рдЮреНрдЪрд╕реНрд╡ (рдкрд┐рддреЗрд╡) рдпрдерд╛ рджрдпрдорд╛рдирдГ рдкрд┐рддрд╛ рддрдерд╛ (рдирдГ) рдЕрд╕реНрдорд╛рдХрдореН (рд╢реГрдгреБрд╣рд┐) (рд╣реВрдпрдорд╛рдирдГ) рдХреГрддрд╛рд╣реНрд╡рд╛рдирдГ рд╕рдиреН рее реп рее

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдерднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - рдкреНрд░рдЬрд╛рд╕реНрдереИрдГ рд╕рднрд╛рдкрддреНрдпрд╛рджрдпреЛ рд░рд╛рдЬрдкреБрд░реБрд╖рд╛ рдЕрдиреНрдирдкрд╛рдирд╡рд╕реНрддреНрд░рдзрдирдпрд╛рдирдордзреБрд░рднрд╛рд╖рдгрд╛рджрд┐рднрд┐рдГ рд╕рджрд╛ рд╣рд░реНрд╖рдпрд┐рддрд╡реНрдпрд╛рдГред рд░рд╛рдЬрдкреБрд░реБрд╖реИрд╢реНрдЪ рдкреНрд░рдЬрд╛рд╕реНрдерд╛рдГ рдкреНрд░рд╛рдгрд┐рдирдГ рдкреБрддреНрд░рд╡рддреНрд╕рддрддрдВ рдкрд╛рд▓рдиреАрдпрд╛ рдЗрддрд┐ рее реп рее рдЕрддреНрд░ рд╕рднрд╛рдкрддреЗ рд░рд╛рдЬреНрдЮрдГ рдкреНрд░рдЬрд╛рдпрд╛рд╢реНрдЪ рдХрд░реНрддреНрддрд╡реНрдпрдХрд░реНрдорд╡рд░реНрдгрдирд╛рджреЗрддрддреНрд╕реВрдХреНрддрд╛рд░реНрдерд╕реНрдп рдкреВрд░реНрд╡рд╕реВрдХреНрддрд╛рд░реНрдереЗрди рд╕рд╣ рд╕рдЩреНрдЧрддрд┐рд░реНрдмреЛрдзреНрдпрд╛ реердЗрддрд┐ рдЪрддреБрд░рдзрд┐рдХрд╢рддрдВ рд╕реВрдХреНрддрдореЗрдХреЛрдирд╡рд┐рдВрд╢реЛ рд╡рд░реНрдЧрд╢реНрдЪ рд╕рдорд╛рдкреНрддрдГ рее

рд╕рд╡рд┐рддрд╛ рдЬреЛрд╢реА тЖР рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рдо) - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдерднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - рдкреНрд░рдЬреЗрдиреЗ рд╕рднрд╛рдкрддреА рдЗрддреНрдпрд╛рджреА рд░рд╛рдЬрдкреБрд░реБрд╖рд╛рдВрдирд╛ рдЦрд╛рди, рдкрд╛рди, рд╡рд╕реНрддреНрд░, рдзрди, рдпрд╛рди рд╡ рдордзреБрд░ рд╡рдЪрдирд╛рдВрдиреА рд╕рджреИрд╡ рдЖрдирдВрджрд┐рдд рдареЗрд╡рд╛рд╡реЗ рд╡ рд░рд╛рдЬрдкреБрд░реБрд╖рд╛рдВрдиреА рдкреНрд░рдЬреЗрд▓рд╛ рдкреБрддреНрд░рд╛рдкреНрд░рдорд╛рдгреЗ рд╡рд╛рдЧрд╡рд╛рд╡реЗ рее реп рее
